It states that in a stationary state, the entropy production inside a thermodynamic system with constant external parameters is minimal and constant. The theorem is significant in the context of non-equilibrium systems and underlines how systems evolve to states of minimum entropy production under certain conditionsââ.William Powers' Perceptual Control Theory (PCT): PCT, developed by William Powers, is based on the properties of negative feedback control loops. It posits that behavior is controlled not by external stimuli, but by internal perceptions. This theory is deeply rooted in biological cybernetics and systems biology, focusing on the control of internal representations of the physical world rather than the external stimuli themselves. PCT differs from other behavioral and cognitive psychology models by emphasizing circular causality and the role of internal perceptions in guiding behaviorââ.Glasser's Choice Theory: Glasser's theory posits that human behavior is centrally driven by five genetically driven needs: survival, love, power, freedom, and fun. The theory emphasizes the importance of fulfilling these needs to achieve psychological well-being. It suggests the existence of a "quality world," a framework of ideal images and beliefs that each individual aspires to, and a "comparing place," where real-world experiences are compared against these ideals. Behavior, according to Glasser, is comprised of acting, thinking, feeling, and physiology, with the first two being within our controlââ.The connection between these theories could be viewed in the light of how internal states (as emphasized in PCT and Glasser's theory) interact with external conditions (a key focus in Prigogine's theorem). While Prigogine's theorem deals with thermodynamic systems, its principles of systems seeking states of minimum entropy can metaphorically parallel the idea of individuals seeking psychological equilibrium and fulfillment as proposed in Glasser's theory. Similarly, the focus on internal perceptions and control in PCT aligns with the internal needs and quality world concepts in Glasser's Choice Theory.However, direct references linking these theories, particularly Prigogine's theorem, to Glasser's Choice Theory are not readily available. The connection, therefore, is more conceptual and interpretative rather than being a direct influence. It's about understanding human behavior and psychological states through the lens of systems theory and internal versus external control dynamics.YouOk the link or dissipative structures in perceptual control theory is indirect but they were related influenced by chaotic attractor theory and cybernetics. They highlight how systems self-organize and evolve, often exhibiting chaotic and unpredictable behavior while still maintaining a form of order.Cybernetics and Feedback Loops: Cybernetics, which significantly influenced the development of PCT, deals with the study of regulatory and feedback systems. It is grounded in the idea of circular causality, where feedback loops are key to maintaining the stability and function of a system. This concept is central to both cybernetics and PCT.Perceptual Control Theory (PCT): Developed by William Powers, PCT applies the principles of negative feedback control loops to behavioral psychology. It posits that behavior is a means of controlling perception, not the end result of stimulus-response chains. PCT is deeply rooted in cybernetics and systems biology, emphasizing the control of internal representations rather than the external stimuli themselves. Powers' work in PCT is directly influenced by the early development of cybernetics and control systems engineeringââââ.The integration of these theories provides a framework for understanding behavior and psychological phenomena through the lens of systems theory. It suggests that just as dissipative structures and chaotic attractors explain the emergence and evolution of complex physical systems, cybernetics and PCT offer insights into the complex, feedback-driven nature of human behavior and cognition. The intersection of these theories underscores a shift from linear causality to a more integrated, systems-oriented view of how internal and external factors interact in dynamic and often unpredictable ways.YouAnd here is the connection between behavior the control of perception and the stations of the mind: 1980-1990 CONTROL THEORY

Glasserâs search for a theoretical base for Reality Therapy took a huge step forward in the eighties when Sam Buchholtz, a friend of Dr. Glasserâs, encouraged him to read William Powersâ book â Behavior: The Control Of Perception â (1973) and, as he himself said, he found the key to much of what he had been looking for. Here were ideas that originated in engineering and were developed by a physics graduate to explain the links between perception and behaviour.

There was much in Powersâ Control Theory that resonated with Glasserâs thinking: Internal control; peopleâs reliance on perceptual phenomena rather than any absolute reality; the role of feedback loops in explaining human behaviour. The thermostat-like functioning of the brain to behave in order to reduce âperceptual discrepancyâ would explain how people behaved in order to meet their needs. Glasser was very impressed, consulted with Powers and then put together his own understanding of Control Theory in âStations of the Mindâ (1981). Although Powers acknowledged that Glasserâs book did present his own ideas accurately, Glasser adapted these ideas to his own specialism and eventually would produce âChoice Theoryâ almost two decades later.

âStations of the Mindâ is Glasserâs most technical work but he now believes that the human brain does not work in such a complex way. Analysis of many different levels of perception, for example, was useful to the research-oriented Powers but Glasser no longer uses these in his work.

By 1984 he was ready to produce his own version of Powersâ ideas adapted to explain Reality Therapy. Although the book was called âControl Theoryâ it was already moving away from Powersâ original concepts and this book was to become a very important milestone in the development of Choice Theory. Rather than trying to explain someone elseâs ideas, Glasser made them his own and then set about explaining his own new version.

ChatGPTChatGPTWilliam Glasser's journey towards developing Choice Theory was significantly influenced by William Powers' book "Behavior: The Control of Perception" (1973). Powers' work, rooted in engineering principles, provided Glasser with insights into the links between perception and behavior, resonating with his own ideas about internal control, the role of feedback loops, and the importance of perceptual phenomena. This led to Glasser's "Stations of the Mind" (1981), where he adapted Powers' concepts to his own field. By 1984, Glasser had developed his own version of these ideas, leading to his book "Control Theory," which laid the groundwork for what would eventually become Choice Theory, marking a significant milestone in its development. Artemy Kolchinsky, Iman Marvian, Can Gokler, Zi-Wen Liu, Peter Shor, Oles Shtanko, Kevin Thompson, David Wolpert, Seth Lloyd
Download PDF
Free energy is energy that is available to do work. Maximizing the free energy gain and the gain in work that can be extracted from a system is important for a wide variety of physical and technological processes, from energy harvesting processes such as photosynthesis to energy storage systems such as fuels and batteries. This paper extends recent results from non-equilibrium thermodynamics and quantum resource theory to derive closed-form solutions for the maximum possible gain in free energy and extractable work that can be obtained by varying the initial states of classical and quantum stochastic processes. Simple formulae allow the comparison the free energy increase for the optimal procedure with that for a sub-optimal procedure. The problem of finding the optimal free-energy harvesting procedure is shown to be convex and solvable via gradient descent.ChatGPTChatGPTThe paper you're referring to discusses the maximization of free energy gain in various systems, both physical and technological. It extends current knowledge in non-equilibrium thermodynamics and quantum resource theory to provide solutions for the maximum possible gain in free energy and work extractable from classical and quantum stochastic processes. The authors present simple formulae for comparing optimal and sub-optimal free energy increase procedures and demonstrate that finding the optimal free-energy harvesting process is a convex problem solvable through gradient descent. This research has broad implications for energy harvesting and storage technologies, including photosynthesis, fuels, and batteries.YouDoes it have any connections to our previous discussion?ChatGPTChatGPTThe paper you mentioned focuses on maximizing free energy gain in physical and technological systems, whereas our previous discussion centered around the psychological concept of minimizing free energy in cognitive models. Corporations use it for personnel management; colleges use it for guidance counseling; individuals use it for self-help.
